<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1444593179">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1444593179" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/36071668-pradnya-k" class="left imgcol" title="Pradnya K."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1447145274p2/36071668.jpg" alt="Pradnya K." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1444593179?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 13, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/36071668-pradnya-k" class="user" title="Pradnya K.">Pradnya K.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/157993.The_Little_Prince" title="Paperback 9780156012195" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Every grownup with child's heart within </span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/36071668-pradnya-k?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/36071668-pradnya-k?shelf=2015" class="actionLinkLite">2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/36071668-pradnya-k?shelf=classic" class="actionLinkLite">classic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/36071668-pradnya-k?shelf=novella" class="actionLinkLite">novella</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/36071668-pradnya-k?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1444593179">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11307161621095074207"><em>“But on your tiny planet, my little prince, all you need do is move your chair a few steps. You can see the day end and the twilight falling whenever you like..”<br /><br />“One day," you said to me, "I saw the sunset forty-four times!”<br />“And a little later you added:<br /><br />"You know-- one loves the sunset, when one is so sad...”</em><br /><br />Those were the lines I read somewhere which pulled strings of my heart. The little boys always do that trick to my heart.<br /><br />The little prince by Antoine de Saint-Exupery, is an allegory, a mult</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11307161621095074207"><em>“But on your tiny planet, my little prince, all you need do is move your chair a few steps. You can see the day end and the twilight falling whenever you like..”<br /><br />“One day," you said to me, "I saw the sunset forty-four times!”<br />“And a little later you added:<br /><br />"You know-- one loves the sunset, when one is so sad...”</em><br /><br />Those were the lines I read somewhere which pulled strings of my heart. The little boys always do that trick to my heart.<br /><br />The little prince by Antoine de Saint-Exupery, is an allegory, a multi layered, very very complicated story, written with simplicity of genius which would fascinate little boys of 6 years and little boys in the 60 years. (Oh, and girls too!)<br /><br />A desert, a plane crash survivor and a tiny little boy, let's call it Little Prince, for he's not from our earth but came from an asteroid, with golden hair and and very inquisitive mind which always finds it amusing how grown ups work. The narrator when he was child was discouraged often from drawing painting and no grown up could get his drawings of boa constrictor from inside and boa constrictor from outside. But after years when his plane crashes in desert he meets this curious little prince who sees his boa at first glance. Surprised narrator makes him drawings of sheep as demanded and unable to produce one to the likings of Prince he draws a box saying the sheep is inside. The little prince immediately likes it!<br />That's the motif of the story. The things seen from curious and awed eyes of little ones. <br /><br /><em>“Children should always show great forbearance toward grown-up people.”</em><br /><br />The narrative learns about the little prince over next few days that he is prince of little planet with three volcanoes which he uses to cook the meal. The volcanoes have to be cleaned else they give you troubles. Elementary, isn't it? And there are baobab trees which should be curbed when they're small else they destroy the planet with their roots. That was for nazism as the story was written by Saint-Exupery when he had to flee from France to USA in 1940. <br />On his planet there's this petulant, delicate and beautiful flowers which he loves immediately. But with its vanity the flower hurts prince and so the prince decides to travel the world around.<br /><br /><em>“This flower is a very complex creature...”<br />“She cast her fragrance and her radiance over me. I ought never to have run away from her... I ought to have guessed all the affection that lay behind her poor little stratagems. Flowers are so inconsistent! But I was too young to know how to love her...”</em><br /><br />Now, wouldn't every man say the same for their love? The flower depicts the real love of writer for his wife. Their relationship was disturbed at times. The flower apologizes and encourages prince to go on his journey. Throughout the book the prince longs to see his flower, thinks of her and wish to protect her. <br /><br />On the journey prince meets a king, a conceited man, a tippler, a lamplighter, a businessman and a geographer. Writer has shown the futility of the grown up through each. The king never orders anything which cannot be obeyed, the geographer doesn't know what he's written, the tippler is ashamed of drinking and to overcome his shame he drinks more. The lamplighter who is lazy and yet follows orders without saying a word. Ah! The tragedy of the world today..<br /><br /> Finally on earth he meets a snake at desert. And then a fox whom he befriends (tames)<br /><br /><em>“if you tame me, it will be as if the sun came to shine on my life. I shall know the sound of a step that will be different from all the others. Other steps send me hurrying back underneath the ground. Yours will call me, like music, out of my burrow.”<br /><br />“Please-- tame me!" he said.”<br />“One only understands the things that one tames," said the fox.”</em><br /><br />And the prince realizes that he's spent time on his flower, he's been tamed by it. <br />On sorrowful parting the fox gift him wisdom.<br /><br /><em>“It is only with the heart that one can see rightly; what is essential is invisible to the eye.”</em><br />Then he sees men, trains on earth, traveling without meaning. Only children enjoy the journey with their nose flattened against window glass. <br /><br />The prince is now tired and thirsty and so is narrator. Together they walk and when prince sleeps, narrator picks up his tender body in his hand and goes in the search of well. On dawn, they find well and drink sweet water of singing well.<br /><br /><em>“What makes the desert beautiful," said the little prince, "is that somewhere it hides a well.”</em><br /><br />At last the prince, being his body to heavy, decides to travel to his star without body. He asks the python to send him to the star. (Did you see death ever described so tenderly?)<br /><br />The prince refuses the narrator to visit him for goodbye for it won't be a pleasant sight for narrator. The laughter of prince will remain with narrator and every time he would see the stars the laughter will come to him. He has millions of laughing stars.<br /><em>“It will be as if, in place of the stars, I had given you a great number of little bells that knew how to laugh...”</em><br /><br />The little prince not only pulls the strings of heart, it also breaks few.<br />It's a short novella I'm going to read several times. Every time it'd be different journey, deep and fragile like the onionskin papers of Saint-Exupery's illustrations and would leave me with the secret gifts.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11307161621095074207">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_425245299">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/425245299" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7242620-boysie-freeman-not-my-real-name-it-s-just-my-internet-name" class="left imgcol" title="Boysie Freeman (not my real name, it's just my Internet name)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1324740403p2/7242620.jpg" alt="Boysie Freeman (not my real name, it's just my Internet name)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/425245299?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 02, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7242620-boysie-freeman-not-my-real-name-it-s-just-my-internet-name" class="user" title="Boysie Freeman (not my real name, it's just my Internet name)">Boysie Freeman (not my real name, it's just my Internet name)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/157993.The_Little_Prince" title="Paperback 9780156012195" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer425245299">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12532090162711144341">Okay so there is this little adorable kid from an asteroid who is seemingly innocent yet amazingly wise.<br /><br />Some people say this book is for children but NO NO NO! THIS BOOK IS NOT FOR CHILDREN. And the Little Prince, I am sorry, he is not a kid. Children will not understand the metaphors and allegories in this tale. Exupery had warned. The Little Prince, let's call him François (typical French name for your convenience) is not a kid, he IS AN ADULT who cannot make any senses of the adult world. (Wh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12532090162711144341">Okay so there is this little adorable kid from an asteroid who is seemingly innocent yet amazingly wise.<br /><br />Some people say this book is for children but NO NO NO! THIS BOOK IS NOT FOR CHILDREN. And the Little Prince, I am sorry, he is not a kid. Children will not understand the metaphors and allegories in this tale. Exupery had warned. The Little Prince, let's call him François (typical French name for your convenience) is not a kid, he IS AN ADULT who cannot make any senses of the adult world. (What kind of kids speak the words from François's mouth, seriously?) <br /><br />*SPOILER BELOW*<br /><br />François is a heart-broken daydreamer who lives forever in his carefree childhood and rejects to grow up because he can only see ugliness and triviality in the adult world. He had a mundane daily job of cleaning volcanoes on his asteroid, <s>his spouse</s> his rose - whom he once admired and loved - began to annoy the shit out of him, and he'd had enough of it, so he decided to travel all the way just to find himself in greater resentment and disdain for the world. As far as I'm concerned, François cannot manage adulthood. During his journey he also met numerous people but none of those could share his interests and ideas - to François those people are too boring, superficial or greedy to begin with.<br /><br />Somehow he made friend with a "wise one" aka Monsieur Fox (He's also an adult) who taught him about love and friendship. This is the best part of the story. François started to realize that he is no way deeper than people around him and how awfully he treated his loved one. François got an emotional breakdown, and started to question his existence, his relationships, and even his own "innocence" - the quality that he once thought, set him aside from those boring, superficial, greedy adults he met in his life. Foreseeing his imminent death, François tried to share his enlightment with a stranded pilot before too late.<br /><br />*END OF SPOILER*<br /><br />I like Le Petit Prince, and how people misunderstood it despite the fact that Exupery had emphasized that it was not written for kids. Mr. E. IS François, and this is his painful experience being with "the grown ups". François is his alter ego/imaginary friend - not a kid. Just my $0.02.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12532090162711144341">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer785749734">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6733432303005499591">I can't even remember the last time I looked up into the sky to see the stars, but <strong>THE LITTLE PRINCE</strong> has reminded me to stop, take a look and perhaps <strong>see</strong> a <em>little</em> more clearly.<p>Having experienced it, I now know why this wonderful <em>little</em> symbolic narrative <em>(for children and adults alike)</em> is so widely read and enjoyed.</p><p>A great <em>little</em> gem of a read to remember always!</p></span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FrAmZr7cVH19demuboWYNuJbmP3QJjJPywij3R1WD8dzZBm1TA5Ah/GtnRUQ5H6Eq6xb5Jn7deWhAaZP0cP5gg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1020792.Antoine_de_Saint_Exup_ry">About Antoine de Saint-Exupéry</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/1020792.Antoine_de_Saint_Exup_ry"><img alt="1020792" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1330853515p2/1020792.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer4031503983396171481">Antoine de Saint-Exupéry was born in Lyons on June 29, 1900. He flew for the first time at the age of twelve, at the Ambérieu airfield, and it was then that he became determined to be a pilot. He kept that ambition even after moving to a school in Switzerland and while spending summer vacations at the family's château at Saint-Maurice-de-Rémens, in eastern France. (The house at Saint-Maurice appea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4031503983396171481">Antoine de Saint-Exupéry was born in Lyons on June 29, 1900. He flew for the first time at the age of twelve, at the Ambérieu airfield, and it was then that he became determined to be a pilot. He kept that ambition even after moving to a school in Switzerland and while spending summer vacations at the family's château at Saint-Maurice-de-Rémens, in eastern France. (The house at Saint-Maurice appears again and again in Saint-Exupéry's writing.)<br /><br />Later, in Paris, he failed the entrance exams for the French naval academy and, instead, enrolled at the prestigious art school l'Ecole des Beaux-Arts. In 1921 Saint-Exupéry began serving in the military, and was stationed in Strasbourg. There he learned to be a pilot, and his career path was forever settled. <br /><br />After leaving the service, in 1923, Saint-Exupéry worked in several professions, but in 1926 he went back to flying and signed on as a pilot for Aéropostale, a private airline that flew mail from Toulouse, France, to Dakar, Senegal. In 1927 Saint-Exupéry accepted the position of airfield chief for Cape Juby, in southern Morocco, and began writing his first book, a memoir called Southern Mail, which was published in 1929. He then moved briefly to Buenos Aires to oversee the establishment of an Argentinean mail service; when he returned to Paris in 1931, he published Night Flight, which won instant success and the prestigious Prix Femina. <br /><br />Always daring, Saint-Exupéry tried in 1935 to break the speed record for flying from Paris to Saigon. Unfortunately, his plane crashed in the Libyan desert, and he and his copilot had to trudge through the sand for three days to find help. In 1938 he was seriously injured in a second plane crash, this time as he tried to fly between New York City and Tierra del Fuego, Argentina. The crash resulted in a long convalescence in New York. <br /><br />Saint-Exupéry's next novel, Wind, Sand and Stars, was published in 1939. A great success, the book won the Académie Française's Grand Prix du Roman (Grand Prize for Novel Writing) and the National Book Award in the United States. At the beginning of the Second World War, Saint-Exupéry flew reconnaissance missions for France, but he went to New York to ask the United States for help when the Germans occupied his country. He drew on his wartime experiences to write Flight to Arras and Letter to a Hostage, both published in 1942. His classic The Little Prince appeared in 1943. Later in 1943 Saint-Exupéry rejoined his French air squadron in northern Africa. Despite being forbidden to fly (he was still suffering physically from his earlier plane crashes), Saint-Exupéry insisted on being given a mission. On July 31, 1944, he set out from Borgo, Corsica, to overfly occupied France. He never returned. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4031503983396171481">...more</a>
